[2026-02-25T16:06:37Z] Chinmay Mehta: Mani (216–274/277 CE) was the Iranian prophet and founder of Manichaeism, a 3rd-century dualistic religion often called the "Religion of Light". He considered himself the final "Apostle of Light" and "Illuminator," succeeding figures like Zoroaster, Buddha, and Jesus to bring a universal, definitive message aimed at liberating divine light trapped in the material world. ↵ Wikipedia ↵ Wikipedia ↵  +3 ↵ Key Aspects of Mani, the Prophet of Light: ↵ The "Religion of Light": Mani’s teachings held that the universe is in an intense battle between light (good/spirit) and darkness (evil/matter). The goal was to liberate particles of light trapped within matter, which would then return to the Kingdom of Light. ↵ The Final Prophet: Mani viewed himself as the "Seal of the Prophets" in a line that included Adam, Seth, Enoch, Noah, Abraham, Zoroaster, Buddha, and Jesus. ↵ Universal Mission: Unlike previous prophets whose teachings were localized, Mani believed his revelation was for the entire world. He preached throughout the Persian Empire and traveled as far as India. ↵ Life and Death: Born in Mesopotamia, Mani received revelations at ages 12 and 24, leading him to start his own faith. He was initially supported by the Sasanian king Shapur I but was later imprisoned and executed by Bahram I, with his death referred to by followers as the "crucifixion" or "Passion of the Illuminator". ↵ Titles: He was known by several titles, including "Apostle of Jesus Christ," the "Paraclete of Truth," and the "Buddha of Light"

[2026-02-25T16:13:32Z] Chinmay Mehta: The _ (古事記, "Records of Ancient Matters" or "An Account of Ancient Matters"), also sometimes read as Furukotofumi[1] or Furukotobumi,[2][a] is an early Japanese chronicle of myths, legends, hymns, genealogies, oral traditions, and semi-historical accounts dating as far back as 641[3] concerning the origin of the Japanese archipelago, the kami, and the Japanese imperial line. It is claimed in its preface to have been composed by Ō no Yasumaro at the request of Empress Genmei in the early 8th century (711–712), and thus is usually considered to be the oldest extant literary work in Japan.[4][5]
